# Java Service Initialization Lifecycle Pattern in Kafka

## Your Task

Find all Java source files in apache/kafka that implement the Startable/Stoppable service lifecycle: the Lifecycle interface (or equivalent), the service startup ordering, and the coordinated shutdown sequence that ensures dependent services stop in reverse order.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the crossorg domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/kafka--0753c489, sg-evals/flink--0cc95fcc, sg-evals/camel--1006f047.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/kafka--0753c489` (apache/kafka)
- `sg-evals/flink--0cc95fcc` (apache/flink)
- `sg-evals/camel--1006f047` (apache/camel)

   375→    @Override
   376→    public void run() {
   377→        try {
   378→            log.info("Herder starting");
   379→            herderThread = Thread.currentThread();
   380→
   381→            try (TickThreadStage stage = new TickThreadStage("initializing and reading to the end of internal topics")) {
   382→                startServices();
   383→            }
   384→
   385→            while (!stopping.get()) {
   386→                tick();
   387→
   388→                if (!isReady()) {
   389→                    ready();
   390→                    log.info("Herder started");
   391→                }
   392→            }
   393→
   394→            recordTickThreadStage("shutting down");
   395→            halt();
   396→
   397→            log.info("Herder stopped");
   398→        } catch (Throwable t) {
   399→            log.error("Uncaught exception in herder work thread, exiting: ", t);
   400→            Utils.closeQuietly(this::stopServices, "herder services");
   401→            Exit.exit(1);
   402→        }
   403→    }

   808→    public void halt() {
   809→        synchronized (this) {
   810→            // Clean up any connectors and tasks that are still running.
   811→            log.info("Stopping connectors and tasks that are still assigned to this worker.");
   812→            worker.stopAndAwaitConnectors();
   813→            worker.stopAndAwaitTasks();
   814→
   815→            // Explicitly fail any outstanding requests so they actually get a response and get an
   816→            // understandable reason for their failure.
   817→            DistributedHerderRequest request = requests.pollFirst();
   818→            while (request != null) {
   819→                request.callback().onCompletion(new ConnectException("Worker is shutting down"), null);
   820→                request = requests.pollFirst();
   821→            }
   822→            stopServices();
   823→        }
   824→    }
   825→

   363→    public void stop() {
   364→        log.info("Stopping REST server");
   365→
   366→        try {
   367→            if (handlers.isRunning()) {
   368→                for (Handler handler : handlers.getHandlers()) {
   369→                    if (handler != null) {
   370→                        Utils.closeQuietly(handler::stop, handler.toString());
   371→                    }
   372→                }
   373→            }
   374→            for (Plugin<ConnectRestExtension> connectRestExtensionPlugin : connectRestExtensionPlugins) {
   375→                try {
   376→                    connectRestExtensionPlugin.close();
   377→                } catch (IOException e) {
   378→                    log.warn("Error while invoking close on {}", connectRestExtensionPlugin.get().getClass(), e);
   379→                }
   380→            }
   381→            jettyServer.stop();
   382→            jettyServer.join();
   383→        } catch (Exception e) {
   384→            throw new ConnectException("Unable to stop REST server", e);
   385→        } finally {
   386→            try {
   387→                jettyServer.destroy();
   388→            } catch (Exception e) {
   389→                log.error("Unable to destroy REST server", e);
   390→            }
   391→        }
   392→
   393→        log.info("REST server stopped");
   394→    }

    38→    void shutdown() throws InterruptedException;

## Summary

I've created `/workspace/answer.json` with detailed findings on the Startable/Stoppable service lifecycle implementation in Apache Kafka. The analysis includes:

### Key Findings:

**42 Java source files** implementing service lifecycle patterns, organized into:

1. **Core Framework**
   - `ShutdownableThread` - Abstract thread lifecycle manager
   - `Scheduler` interface - Task scheduling contract
   - `KafkaScheduler` - Thread pool implementation

2. **Service Startup Chain** (Connect module as primary example):
   - `Connect.start()` → `Herder.start()` → `startServices()` → Service cascade
   - `AbstractHerder.startServices()` starts: Worker → StatusBackingStore → ConfigBackingStore

3. **Coordinated Shutdown Sequence** (reverse order):
   - `Connect.stop()` → `Herder.stop()` → `halt()` → `stopServices()`
   - `AbstractHerder.stopServices()` stops: StatusBackingStore → ConfigBackingStore → Worker

4. **Lifecycle Patterns Identified**:
   - `ShutdownableThread` subclasses (5 implementations)
   - `Scheduler` interface implementations
   - Herder hierarchy (DistributedHerder, StandaloneHerder)
   - Backing store services (ConfigBackingStore, OffsetBackingStore, StatusBackingStore)
   - Coordinator services (GroupCoordinatorService, ShareCoordinatorService)
   - Raft and log services with lifecycle management
   - Stream processing threads with state machines

5. **Synchronization Mechanisms**:
   - CountDownLatches for coordination
   - AtomicBoolean for state flags
   - ReentrantLocks for critical sections

It is possible that\n     * an operation is completed after it has been added to the watch list for some, but\n     * not all the keys. In this case, the operation is considered completed and won't\n     * be added to the watch list of the remaining keys. The expiration reaper thread will\n     * remove this operation from any watcher list in which the operation exists.\n     *\n     * @param operation the delayed operation to be checked\n     * @param watchKeys keys for bookkeeping the operation\n     * @return true iff the delayed operations can be completed by the caller\n     */\n    public <K extends DelayedOperationKey> boolean tryCompleteElseWatch(T operation, List<K> watchKeys) {\n        if (watchKeys.isEmpty()) {\n            throw new IllegalArgumentException(\"The watch key list can't be empty\");\n        }\n\n        // The cost of tryComplete() is typically proportional to the number of keys. Calling tryComplete() for each key is\n        // going to be expensive if there are many keys. Instead, we do the check in the following way through safeTryCompleteOrElse().\n        // If the operation is not completed, we just add the operation to all keys. Then we call tryComplete() again. At\n        // this time, if the operation is still not completed, we are guaranteed that it won't miss any future triggering\n        // event since the operation is already on the watcher list for all keys.\n        //\n        // ==============[story about lock]==============\n        // Through safeTryCompleteOrElse(), we hold the operation's lock while adding the operation to watch list and doing\n        // the tryComplete() check. This is to avoid a potential deadlock between the callers to tryCompleteElseWatch() and\n        // checkAndComplete(). For example, the following deadlock can happen if the lock is only held for the final tryComplete()\n        // 1) thread_a holds readlock of stateLock from TransactionStateManager\n        // 2) thread_a is executing tryCompleteElseWatch()\n        // 3) thread_a adds op to watch list\n        // 4) thread_b requires writelock of stateLock from TransactionStateManager (blocked by thread_a)\n        // 5) thread_c calls checkAndComplete() and holds lock of op\n        // 6) thread_c is waiting readlock of stateLock to complete op (blocked by thread_b)\n        // 7) thread_a is waiting lock of op to call the final tryComplete() (blocked by thread_c)\n        //\n        // Note that even with the current approach, deadlocks could still be introduced. For example,\n        // 1) thread_a calls tryCompleteElseWatch() and gets lock of op\n        // 2) thread_a adds op to watch list\n        // 3) thread_a calls op#tryComplete and tries to require lock_b\n        // 4) thread_b holds lock_b and calls checkAndComplete()\n        // 5) thread_b sees op from watch list\n        // 6) thread_b needs lock of op\n        // To avoid the above scenario, we recommend DelayedOperationPurgatory.checkAndComplete() be called without holding\n        // any exclusive lock. Since DelayedOperationPurgatory.checkAndComplete() completes delayed operations asynchronously,\n        // holding an exclusive lock to make the call is often unnecessary.\n        if (operation.safeTryCompleteOrElse(() -> {\n            watchKeys.forEach(key -> {\n                if (!operation.isCompleted())\n                    watchForOperation(key, operation);\n            });\n            if (!watchKeys.isEmpty())\n                estimatedTotalOperations.incrementAndGet();\n        })) {\n            return true;\n        }\n\n        // if it cannot be completed by now and hence is watched, add to the timeout queue also\n        if (!operation.isCompleted()) {\n            if (timerEnabled)\n                timeoutTimer.add(operation);\n            if (operation.isCompleted()) {\n                // cancel the timer task\n                operation.cancel();\n            }\n        }\n        return false;\n    }\n\n    /**\n     * Check if some delayed operations can be completed with the given watch key,\n     * and if yes complete them.\n     *\n     * @return the number of completed operations during this process\n     */\n    public <K extends DelayedOperationKey> int checkAndComplete(K key) {\n        WatcherList wl = watcherList(key);\n        Watchers watchers;\n        wl.watchersLock.lock();\n        try {\n            watchers = wl.watchersByKey.get(key);\n        } finally {\n            wl.watchersLock.unlock();\n        }\n        int numCompleted = watchers == null ? 0 : watchers.tryCompleteWatched();\n\n        if (numCompleted > 0) {\n            LOG.debug(\"Request key {} unblocked {} {} operations\", key, numCompleted, purgatoryName);\n        }\n        return numCompleted;\n    }\n\n    /**\n     * Return the total size of watch lists the purgatory. All the \"real\" work of the herder is\n        // performed in this thread, which keeps synchronization straightforward at the cost of some operations possibly\n        // blocking up this thread (especially those in callbacks due to rebalance events).\n\n        try {\n            // if we failed to read to end of log before, we need to make sure the issue was resolved before joining group\n            // Joining and immediately leaving for failure to read configs is exceedingly impolite\n            if (!canReadConfigs) {\n                if (readConfigToEnd(workerSyncTimeoutMs)) {\n                    canReadConfigs = true;\n                } else {\n                    return; // Safe to return and tick immediately because readConfigToEnd will do the backoff for us\n                }\n            }\n\n            log.debug(\"Ensuring group membership is still active\");\n            String stageDescription = \"ensuring membership in the cluster\";\n            member.ensureActive(() -> new TickThreadStage(stageDescription));\n            completeTickThreadStage();\n            // Ensure we're in a good state in our group. If not restart and everything should be setup to rejoin\n            if (!handleRebalanceCompleted()) return;\n        } catch (WakeupException e) {\n            // May be due to a request from another thread, or might be stopping. If the latter, we need to check the\n            // flag immediately. If the former, we need to re-run the ensureActive call since we can't handle requests\n            // unless we're in the group.\n            log.trace(\"Woken up while ensure group membership is still active\");\n            return;\n        }\n\n        if (fencedFromConfigTopic) {\n            if (isLeader()) {\n                // We were accidentally fenced out, possibly by a zombie leader\n                try {\n                    log.debug(\"Reclaiming write privileges for config topic after being fenced out\");\n                    try (TickThreadStage stage = new TickThreadStage(\"reclaiming write privileges for the config topic\")) {\n                        configBackingStore.claimWritePrivileges();\n                    }\n                    fencedFromConfigTopic = false;",

48\u2192\n    49\u2192/**\n    50\u2192 * The cleaner is responsible for removing obsolete records from logs which have the \"compact\" retention strategy.\n    51\u2192 * A message with key K and offset O is obsolete if there exists a message with key K and offset O' such that O < O'.\n    52\u2192 * <p>\n    53\u2192 * Each log can be thought of being split into two sections of segments: a \"clean\" section which has previously been cleaned followed by a\n    54\u2192 * \"dirty\" section that has not yet been cleaned. The dirty section is further divided into the \"cleanable\" section followed by an \"uncleanable\" section.\n    55\u2192 * The uncleanable section is excluded from cleaning. The active log segment is always uncleanable. If there is a\n    56\u2192 * compaction lag time set, segments whose largest message timestamp is within the compaction lag time of the cleaning operation are also uncleanable.\n    57\u2192 * <p>\n    58\u2192 * The cleaning is carried out by a pool of background threads. Each thread chooses the dirtiest log that has the \"compact\" retention policy\n    59\u2192 * and cleans that. The dirtiness of the log is guessed by taking the ratio of bytes in the dirty section of the log to the total bytes in the log.\n    60\u2192 * <p>\n    61\u2192 * To clean a log the cleaner first builds a mapping of key=>last_offset for the dirty section of the log. See {@link OffsetMap} for details of\n    62\u2192 * the implementation of the mapping.\n    63\u2192 * <p>\n    64\u2192 * Once the key=>last_offset map is built, the log is cleaned by recopying each log segment but omitting any key that appears in the offset map with a\n    65\u2192 * higher offset than what is found in the segment (i.e. messages with a key that appears in the dirty section of the log).\n    66\u2192 * <p>\n    67\u2192 * To avoid segments shrinking to very small sizes with repeated cleanings we implement a rule by which if we will merge successive segments when\n    68\u2192 * doing a cleaning if their log and index size are less than the maximum log and index size prior to the clean beginning.\n    69\u2192 * <p>\n    70\u2192 * Cleaned segments are swapped into the log as they become available.\n    71\u2192 * <p>\n    72\u2192 * One nuance that the cleaner must handle is log truncation. If a log is truncated while it is being cleaned the cleaning of that log is aborted.\n    73\u2192 * <p>\n    74\u2192 * Messages with null payload are treated as deletes for the purpose of log compaction. This means that they receive special treatment by the cleaner.\n    75\u2192 * The cleaner will only retain delete records for a period of time to avoid accumulating space indefinitely. This period of time is configurable on a per-topic\n    76\u2192 * basis and is measured from the time the segment enters the clean portion of the log (at which point any prior message with that key has been removed).\n    77\u2192 * Delete markers in the clean section of the log that are older than this time will not be retained when log segments are being recopied as part of cleaning.\n    78\u2192 * This time is tracked by setting the base timestamp of a record batch with delete markers when the batch is recopied in the first cleaning that encounters\n    79\u2192 * it. The relative timestamps of the records in the batch are also modified when recopied in this cleaning according to the new base timestamp of the batch.\n    80\u2192 * <p>\n    81\u2192 * Note that cleaning is more complicated with the idempotent/transactional producer capabilities. 40\u2192\n    41\u2192/**\n    42\u2192 * <p>\n    43\u2192 * The herder interface tracks and manages workers and connectors. It is the main interface for external components\n    44\u2192 * to make changes to the state of the cluster. For example, in distributed mode, an implementation of this interface\n    45\u2192 * knows how to accept a connector configuration, may need to route it to the current leader worker for the cluster so\n    46\u2192 * the config can be written to persistent storage, and then ensures the new connector is correctly instantiated on one\n    47\u2192 * of the workers.\n    48\u2192 * </p>\n    49\u2192 * <p>\n    50\u2192 * This class must implement all the actions that can be taken on the cluster (add/remove connectors, pause/resume tasks,\n    51\u2192 * get state of connectors and tasks, etc). In that case,\n    56\u2192 * the implementation will mainly be delegating tasks directly to other components. For example, when creating a new\n    57\u2192 * connector in standalone mode, there is no need to persist the config and the connector and its tasks must run in the\n    58\u2192 * same process, so the standalone herder implementation can immediately instantiate and start the connector and its\n    59\u2192 * tasks.\n    60\u2192 * </p>\n    61\u2192 */\n    62\u2192public interface Herder {\n    63\u2192\n    64\u2192    void start();\n    65\u2192\n    66\u2192    void stop();\n    67\u2192\n    68\u2192    /**\n    69\u2192     * @return whether the worker is ready; i.e., it has completed all initialization and startup\n    70\u2192     * steps such as creating internal topics, joining a cluster, etc.\n    71\u2192     */\n    72\u2192    boolean isReady();\n    73\u2192\n    74\u2192    /**\n    75\u2192     * Check for worker health; i.e., its ability to service external requests from the user such\n    76\u2192     * as creating, reconfiguring, and deleting connectors\n    77\u2192     * @param callback callback to invoke once worker health is assured\n    78\u2192     */\n    79\u2192    void healthCheck(Callback<Void> callback);\n    80\u2192\n    81\u2192    /**\n    82\u2192     * Get a list of connectors currently running in this cluster. initialization and lifecycle management pattern across its codebase. The pattern consists of three key layers:\\n\\n## 1. Core Lifecycle Framework\\n\\nThe foundation is built on abstract base classes and interfaces:\\n\\n- **ShutdownableThread** (server-common/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/server/util/ShutdownableThread.java): Abstract base class for lifecycle-managed background threads. Provides coordinated shutdown through `shutdown()`, `initiateShutdown()`, and `awaitShutdown()` methods with two CountDownLatches (shutdownInitiated, shutdownComplete) for synchronization. This is extended by multiple worker threads including KafkaRaftClientDriver, InterBrokerSendThread, and TimingWheelExpirationService.\\n\\n- **Scheduler Interface** (server-common/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/server/util/Scheduler.java): Defines the contract for task scheduling services with `startup()` and `shutdown()` methods. Implemented by KafkaScheduler and SystemScheduler for managing thread pools and scheduled task execution.\\n\\n## 2. Service Ordering and Startup Chain\\n\\nKafka Connect demonstrates the hierarchical startup ordering through the **Connect** class (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/Connect.java). The startup sequence is:\\n\\n1. Connect.start() initiates the startup\\n2. Herder.start() starts the herder (either DistributedHerder or StandaloneHerder)\\n3. DistributedHerder.startServices() is called internally via the main thread\\n4. AbstractHerder.startServices() orchestrates dependent service startup in order:\\n   - Worker.start() - initializes the worker\\n   - StatusBackingStore.start() - starts the status store\\n   - ConfigBackingStore.start() - starts the config store\\n5. RestServer.initializeResources() - initializes REST resources\\n\\n## 3. Coordinated Shutdown Sequence\\n\\nThe shutdown follows the reverse dependency order through **DistributedHerder**:\\n\\n1. Connect.stop() is called\\n2. Herder.stop() is triggered\\n3. DistributedHerder.halt() executes:\\n   - Stops all running connectors and tasks\\n   - Clears pending requests\\n   - Calls stopServices()\\n4. DistributedHerder.stopServices() delegates to:\\n5. AbstractHerder.stopServices() shuts down in reverse order:\\n   - StatusBackingStore.stop()\\n   - ConfigBackingStore.stop()\\n   - Worker.stop()\\n6. RestServer.stop() closes the REST interface\\n7. ExecutorService shutdown for herderExecutor, forwardRequestExecutor, and startAndStopExecutor\\n\\n## 4. State Management and Synchronization\\n\\nThe lifecycle uses multiple synchronization mechanisms:\\n\\n- **CountDownLatches**: Used in Connect (startLatch, stopLatch) and ShutdownableThread to coordinate threads\\n- **AtomicBoolean**: Used in DistributedHerder (stopping flag) and ConnectMetrics for thread-safe state\\n- **ReentrantLocks**: Used in DelayedOperationPurgatory for watcher list protection\\n\\n## 5. Specialized Service Lifecycles\\n\\n**Coordinator Services** (GroupCoordinatorService, ShareCoordinatorService):\\n- Implement startup() and shutdown() methods\\n- Manage coordinator state machines\\n- Handle graceful shutdown with timeout configurations\\n\\n**Backing Store Services** (KafkaConfigBackingStore, KafkaOffsetBackingStore, KafkaStatusBackingStore):\\n- Implement start() and stop() methods\\n- Manage Kafka client lifecycle\\n- Coordinate with KafkaBasedLog for persistent storage\\n\\n**Raft Services** (KafkaRaftClientDriver, TimingWheelExpirationService):\\n- Extend ShutdownableThread\\n- Implement doWork() for main thread loop\\n- Use initiateShutdown()/awaitShutdown() for graceful termination\\n\\n**Log Services** (LogCleaner):\\n- Manage multiple CleanerThread instances (extends ShutdownableThread)\\n- Support startup() and shutdown() with thread pool coordination\\n- Implement BrokerReconfigurable for dynamic reconfiguration\\n\\n**Stream Services** (StreamThread, GlobalStreamThread):\\n- Extend Thread directly with state machines\\n- Manage stream topology execution\\n- Coordinate task lifecycle through state transitions\\n\\nAll service implementations follow a consistent pattern: initialization allocates resources, startup activates processing threads, and shutdown gracefully terminates in reverse dependency order. The pattern ensures that dependent services are available when needed and cleanly released in the correct order, preventing resource leaks and data corruption.\"\n}\n",

initialization and lifecycle management pattern across its codebase. The pattern consists of three key layers:\\n\\n## 1. Core Lifecycle Framework\\n\\nThe foundation is built on abstract base classes and interfaces:\\n\\n- **ShutdownableThread** (server-common/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/server/util/ShutdownableThread.java): Abstract base class for lifecycle-managed background threads. Provides coordinated shutdown through `shutdown()`, `initiateShutdown()`, and `awaitShutdown()` methods with two CountDownLatches (shutdownInitiated, shutdownComplete) for synchronization. This is extended by multiple worker threads including KafkaRaftClientDriver, InterBrokerSendThread, and TimingWheelExpirationService.\\n\\n- **Scheduler Interface** (server-common/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/server/util/Scheduler.java): Defines the contract for task scheduling services with `startup()` and `shutdown()` methods. Implemented by KafkaScheduler and SystemScheduler for managing thread pools and scheduled task execution.\\n\\n## 2. Service Ordering and Startup Chain\\n\\nKafka Connect demonstrates the hierarchical startup ordering through the **Connect** class (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/Connect.java). The startup sequence is:\\n\\n1. Connect.start() initiates the startup\\n2. Herder.start() starts the herder (either DistributedHerder or StandaloneHerder)\\n3. DistributedHerder.startServices() is called internally via the main thread\\n4. AbstractHerder.startServices() orchestrates dependent service startup in order:\\n   - Worker.start() - initializes the worker\\n   - StatusBackingStore.start() - starts the status store\\n   - ConfigBackingStore.start() - starts the config store\\n5. RestServer.initializeResources() - initializes REST resources\\n\\n## 3. Coordinated Shutdown Sequence\\n\\nThe shutdown follows the reverse dependency order through **DistributedHerder**:\\n\\n1. Connect.stop() is called\\n2. Herder.stop() is triggered\\n3. DistributedHerder.halt() executes:\\n   - Stops all running connectors and tasks\\n   - Clears pending requests\\n   - Calls stopServices()\\n4. DistributedHerder.stopServices() delegates to:\\n5. AbstractHerder.stopServices() shuts down in reverse order:\\n   - StatusBackingStore.stop()\\n   - ConfigBackingStore.stop()\\n   - Worker.stop()\\n6. RestServer.stop() closes the REST interface\\n7. ExecutorService shutdown for herderExecutor, forwardRequestExecutor, and startAndStopExecutor\\n\\n## 4. State Management and Synchronization\\n\\nThe lifecycle uses multiple synchronization mechanisms:\\n\\n- **CountDownLatches**: Used in Connect (startLatch, stopLatch) and ShutdownableThread to coordinate threads\\n- **AtomicBoolean**: Used in DistributedHerder (stopping flag) and ConnectMetrics for thread-safe state\\n- **ReentrantLocks**: Used in DelayedOperationPurgatory for watcher list protection\\n\\n## 5. Specialized Service Lifecycles\\n\\n**Coordinator Services** (GroupCoordinatorService, ShareCoordinatorService):\\n- Implement startup() and shutdown() methods\\n- Manage coordinator state machines\\n- Handle graceful shutdown with timeout configurations\\n\\n**Backing Store Services** (KafkaConfigBackingStore, KafkaOffsetBackingStore, KafkaStatusBackingStore):\\n- Implement start() and stop() methods\\n- Manage Kafka client lifecycle\\n- Coordinate with KafkaBasedLog for persistent storage\\n\\n**Raft Services** (KafkaRaftClientDriver, TimingWheelExpirationService):\\n- Extend ShutdownableThread\\n- Implement doWork() for main thread loop\\n- Use initiateShutdown()/awaitShutdown() for graceful termination\\n\\n**Log Services** (LogCleaner):\\n- Manage multiple CleanerThread instances (extends ShutdownableThread)\\n- Support startup() and shutdown() with thread pool coordination\\n- Implement BrokerReconfigurable for dynamic reconfiguration\\n\\n**Stream Services** (StreamThread, GlobalStreamThread):\\n- Extend Thread directly with state machines\\n- Manage stream topology execution\\n- Coordinate task lifecycle through state transitions\\n\\nAll service implementations follow a consistent pattern: initialization allocates resources, startup activates processing threads, and shutdown gracefully terminates in reverse dependency order.
